2646	1	The masks are made of plastic. And this is a problem.
2646	3	It is estimated that by 2020 approximately 1.6 billion weapons used and thrown into the oceans, more than 4 million per day. By the end of 2021, the world had generated approximately 8 million tons of COVID-related plastic waste, mostly surgical machinery and N95, with approximately 26,000 tons finished in the sea. Many environmental organizations, according to W. Aaron Vandiver, writer, lawyer and environmentalist of wildlife, have not realized this reality or are rejecting to talk too loud about the problem. During the first year of the pandemic, health experts and green groups told us to stick to reusable dust coatings (or to create one from an old shirt). With the rise of the most contagious omicron variant, few have talked about the environmental impacts of the N95 masks. It can be understood that the planet has gone to a second level compared to immediate concerns for human health and safety. And it didn’t help the fact that the debates on masks have become so controversial. But now that state and local governments are withdrawing mandates as the Omicron cases are decreasing, we have to start a conversation on the pollution of the machinery.
2646	5	One unfortunate but inevitable fact is that the surgical masks and N95 (and individual protection devices and test kit) are single-use or, in the best of the cases, double or triple-use plastic. The surgical masks and most respirators are made from polypropylene, a synthetic plastic fiber that, like all plastic, is generated from oil. Although they can and are often used more than once, experts do not recommend wearing them for more than two or three days, says Vandiver on grist.org.
2646	7	So the 400 million N95 masks that the Biden administration has recently begun to put available to Americans, and the millions of “high quality” masks that it is distributing to children, will have a maximum of a few days of use before it ends in the dirt. From there, many will end in the discharges, where they release toxic chemicals into underground waters, or they will be burned, releasing carbon dioxide and toxic chemicals. Many others will end in the rivers and the oceans and make a way into the holes of sea birds such as the Laysan albatro, who mistakenly feed themselves of plastic and damage their pulcs. This aggravates a already alarming problem, as about 90 percent of sea birds are believed to have already ingested plastic in some form. The insidious material will also be eaten by the whales, some of whom have the stomach full of plastic waste. According to some estimates, plastic in the oceans of the world could overcome fish by 2050, a problem that masks will only make it worse.
2646	9	According to the Marine Conservation Organization OceansAsia, ocean masks can take up to 450 years to break, creating smaller plastic pieces in the process. These microplastics have an increasing impact on ocean microorganisms and accumulate in the food chain; a 2018 study found that 100% of the marine turtles examined had microplastics in the digestive system.Other thing to consider: like all single-use plastic, masks increase greenhouse gas emissions at any time of their life cycle. Masks cannot be banned like other single-use plastics, of course, but we must recognize that they have the same devastating effect. For the benefit of the planet, we must take them into account in our personal decisions about the opportunity to wear one, and politicians must take them into account and evaluate the advantages and disadvantages of the masking obligations. Environmentalists may be discouraging to participate in these disputes, but we should look for ways to use fewer plastic masks without compromising their legitimate and necessary use.
2646	10	Recycling of masks
2646	12	It might be annoying to think that we can recycle the masks, or that there is some other easy solution, but the waste management generated from plastic products is an incredibly complex global problem without “a unique solution”, according to a 2020 Pew Trust charity study. Even before the COVID, Pew predicted that the plastic pollution that flows into the oceans will triple by 2040, in the absence of major regulatory and economic reforms worldwide. Recycling does not keep single-use plastic outside the oceans, or outside the discharges and incinerators, and it is estimated that 91% of the goods produced with that stuff are not recycled at all. 
2646	14	Researchers at Cornell University have recently chosen a plan to destroy masks and other DPIs, then heat those smaller pieces to break them into useful chemicals such as ethylene, propylene, propane and butan. To significantly reduce plastic waste, this method should be implemented by states and countries on a wide scale, but this type of “chemical recycling” has been criticized for causing carbon emissions and the release of toxic chemicals into the atmosphere. It is also expensive.
2646	15	What about the N95 reusable?
2646	17	A MIT team has studied possible ways to decontaminate and reuse the N95 masks, potentially making them from silicone, but so far we have not seen such technologies implemented on a large scale and it is not clear whether they are practical solutions. The fact remains that “the single-use plastic mask is just another additional burden that we are leaving to future generations,” as said Gary Stokes, co-founder of OceansAsia.
2646	19	While a number of states have recently announced plans to put an end or reduce the machinery mandates, what happens when the next variant is presented?
2646	21	Those of us who focus on the state of the planet must contribute to the formation of a more sustainable approach in the long term. Government officials and public health experts will continue to discuss the recommendations, but environmentalists can and should defend the planet. Although the N95s are widely used, the good news is that they can provide one-way protection, which means that those who wear it can protect themselves even when others around them are disguised. A more limited and righteous approach to the use of surgical masks and N95 – encouraging use in clinical contexts and to protect people with COVID-19 vulnerabilities by disturbing overuse among healthy and asymptomatic people – can reasonably balance public health priorities with environmental concerns, and potentially prevent a large number of plastic substances from polluting the world. Apart from the big technological innovations, the use of less plastic masks is the best we can do.
2646	23	Now that cases are falling and obligations, as emergency measures are ending (at least for now), we have difficult questions: in what circumstances is it justified to wear, or to ask others to wear, masks harmful to the planet? How much damage will the oceans, marine life and climate be caused if millions or billions of people continue to wear them regularly? How many damages have we already done? What will help to minimize the damage in the future?
2646	25	In any way you decide to use the mask, we will all have to make accounts with the environmental heritage for the coming years. This is the first time we start the conversation, it’s better, concludes Vandiver.265

This is the result of the survey, conducted on a sample of 2,000 citizens, carried out by The European House -Ambrosetti and the Interdepartmental Center for Ethics and Integrity in Research of the National Research Council (Cnr), in collaboration with Swg, presented in Rome during an event carried out by The European House -Ambrosetti with the non-conditional contribution of Pfizer. 
2663	9	According to the survey, 92% believe that vaccines are a safe and effective health tool to counter infectious diseases and that for 33% of respondents the level of confidence during the pandemic has increased, especially in men, in the Southern Regions and among the Generation Z. Positive signs also for the recovery of the anti-Covid vaccine campaign in the coming autumn: 77% declare themselves favorable to the fourth dose of the anti-Covid vaccine in the coming autumn and 17% of those who have not yet vaccinated are open to do so. Incentive also data on flu vaccination with 95% of the subjects who have been vaccinated in the last season that declares favorable to repeat the administration. It also revealed that 88% of respondents feel informed about vaccines, prediling as information sources their doctor’s trust, the opinion of scientists and institutional websites; informal information channels, such as friends and relatives and social media/forum/blog, remain the most used sources by those who are more skeptical about vaccines.
2663	11	The knowledge of compulsory vaccines is also good, the knowledge of other recommended vaccines is poor: 98% of respondents say they are aware of the existence of compulsory vaccines in the pediatric age but only 76% remember at least some of them, values that fall to 94% respectively and 63% for recommended vaccines in the childhood. In both cases, among the most informed are people with minor children and women.
2663	13	The law on the obligatory vaccination for the 0-16 year range and the numerous awareness initiatives carried out, have contributed to increasing the level of knowledge of pediatric vaccinations with resulting coverage rates that, although decreasing during the pandemic, are shown at values greater than 90%. The situation of vaccinations in adolescence and adulthood is different. Only 34% said they were vaccinated against the papilloma virus (in the age range of 18-30 years), only 28% against pneumococcus (in the age range of 60-70 years), only 11% against herpes zoster (in the age range of 60-70 years). “The survey provides some important indications on the leaves on which to act to improve the knowledge and, consequently, the confidence of citizens towards vaccination prevention,” says Daniela Bianco, partner and head of the Healthcare area of The European House – Ambrosetti. “In addition, 1 out of 2 among the exhibitors and opposed to some vaccines such as the anti-pneumococcus, anti-herpes-zoster and anti-papilloma virus is declared available to know more about it before you can decide. Therefore, it is necessary to increase the efforts in communication to increase the level of knowledge of citizens, including on these vaccines.”  And again: 83% of respondents say they know people who hesitate or refuse to vaccinate themselves: if 26% refuse or refuse to vaccinate themselves for all vaccines in general, 57% are only referring to the anti-Covid vaccine. The vaccine barriers perceived are essentially: the fear of health risks and the information gap (not correct or missing information). Even among the subjects who have declared they do not consider vaccines safe and effective and the non-vaccinated against Covid the safety-related fear is confirmed the main reason for non-vaccination, followed by doubts regarding the experiments.
2663	15	Among the tools that, according to the citizens themselves, can help to increase the level of confidence in vaccination programs, include: greater dialogue with their own doctor or trusted pharmacist, greater transparency of the institutions that take care of health and more frequent information campaigns by the health institutions; greater dialogue and better information are considered the most effective tools also to make ideas change to people who hesitate or refuse to vaccinate themselves.  The increase in vaccination points also enables citizens to get closer to vaccination prevention: especially the pharmacies but also the places of study and work, are the most appreciated; even among those who are less favourable to vaccines or have not vaccinated against Covid the pharmacy is a suitable place for vaccination.  “55% of the respondents also open to economic incentives, in primis check up and free medical examinations and the bonuses for gyms and sports centers, tools that in addition to increasing the level of citizens’ confidence also have a role in activating economic activity,” says Andrea Grignolio, responsible for the Vaccine Hesitancy Forum of the Interdipartimental Center for Ethics and Integrity in the Research of the Cnr. “These are propositive instruments aimed at achieving greater vaccine accession that could be linked to those already in use such as disincentive and penalties and that would align Italian vaccine policies with those promoted by many other countries.”

2674	1	Why do people eat meat even if it affects the planet and their health?
2674	3	We know that today’s meat and dairy industries damage our planet and that eating too much animal protein can be harmful to our health. Why do we eat meat?
2674	5	Human beings have eaten meat since prehistory, but only in the last 50 years the world’s meat production has quadruplicated, reaching about 350 million tons per year, according to the United Nations Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO).
2674	7	By 2050, global meat production is expected to reach 455 million tons.
2674	8	The source of inefficient cibo
2674	10	Scientists have long expressed concern about the environmental impacts of meat consumption, especially industrially produced meat, because it requires more energy, water and land than any other food source, making it “ineffective.”
2674	12	A study on agriculture’s environmental impact revealed that beef production is responsible for six times more greenhouse gas emissions and 36 times more soil use than plant protein production.
2674	13	Therefore, avoiding meat and dairy is the best way to reduce our environmental impact on the planet, the study concludes. The global exploitation of agricultural land could thus be reduced to more than 75%.
2674	14	Psychological reasons for meat consumption
2674	16	Dr. Benjamin Buttlar, a social psychologist at the University of Treviri, believes that many people eat meat according to their cultural habits.
2674	17	“I think many people simply appreciate the taste of meat. The other problem is the identity of the food. Many traditional cuisine revolve around certain meat dishes,” he told DW, adding that we often don’t even question what we have: “Most times, these habits prevent us from thinking that meat consumption is really negative, because it’s something we always do.”
2674	19	Another important aspect is that when we eat meat, we do not associate it with the suffering of animals. Only when we talk to vegetarians, vegans, or when we see a documentary about the well-being of animals, we justify it by saying that man has eaten meat for an unforgettable time.
2674	21	Especially men are more justified when they eat meat saying that it is a natural, normal and necessary part of our diet. Buttlar explained that “there are more young women who want to be vegetarians than men, because eating meat remains a male stereotype, responding to the image of strong men who hunt, and is linked to evolutionary misideas on meat consumption.”
2674	22	The hypothesis is that “the flesh has left us human.”
2674	24	For a long time, researchers have believed that eating meat helped our ancestors to develop more physical characteristics similar to those of humans, and the consumption of meat and bone marrow provided Homo erectus the energy it needed to grow and feed a larger brain.
2674	26	But a new study has questioned the priority of meat consumption in our evolution. The authors of this research have argued that, although archaeological evidence of meat consumption has increased with the appearance of Homo erectus, this may have happened because the subject has been thoroughly studied and more bone remains have been sought consumed by man during that period.
2674	27	by EADV
2674	29	“I was definitely very surprised by this discovery,” said Briana Pobiner, a paleoantropologist at the Smithsonian Museum of Natural History and co-author of the study, “I was one of those people who, for a long time, had the narrative that Homo erectus evolved because the meat consumption increased, so these discoveries forced me to re-think my perception of our evolutionary history.”
2674	30	Currently, 75% of the world’s food comes from 12 species of vegetables and five animals. But if we consume too much of a single food source, it can cause health problems.
2674	32	“A number of studies show that animal protein consumption is related to the development of a variety of cancers,” said Dr. Milton Mills, internist and specialist in intensive therapy in the United States.
2674	34	Mills, a vegetable-based diet advocate and founder of a vegetable-based diet website, does not agree that vegetarian and vegans lack certain proteins and nutrients.
2674	36	“These theories were born 50 or 60 years ago, when people mistakenly believed that meat was somehow more nutritious than vegetables. It was a wrong idea that people had, that there are only some amino acids that can be obtained from animals. And that’s not all true,” Mills said.
2674	38	 If the world’s population will continue with its current meat appetite, it will no longer be able to meet food demand by 2050, when there will be 10 billion inhabitants on the planet.
2674	40	But how can we reduce global meat consumption levels? “For example, make meat-based products expensive as they should be for the well-being of animals and, in terms of cost, for the climate. And make the alternatives cheaper,” says Benjamin Buttlar. It is also important that people have positive culinary experiences with vegetables, he says. Instead of saying “You shouldn’t eat meat,” people should be encouraged to try different dishes without meat-based products, she explains.
